  <resource id="10.119">Lowbrow/highbrow bifurcation, anyone?</resource>
  <resource id="10.120">To pencil in those few vacant nanosecs I read Dennis Perrin's Mr. Mike: The Life and Work of Michael O'Donoghue.</resource>
  <resource id="10.121">(I'd romped and stabbed through Don Fleming's copy three years ago, but I found one online for $6 and couldn't resist.)</resource>
  <resource id="10.122">Earlier, I sped past the finale of Frank Rich's The Greatest Story Ever Sold.</resource>
  <resource id="10.123">My head is a WMD, and my hard drive is naught but night soil.</resource>
  <resource id="10.124">Sadness Dept: Just heard on NPR that Gillo Pontecorvo died.</resource>
  <resource id="10.125">Drat.</resource>
  <resource id="10.126">Click here for the New York Times' obit.</resource>
  <resource id="10.127">He directed the seminal Battle of Algiers and shortly thereafter, Burn!, which starred Marlon Brando.</resource>
  <resource id="10.128">His 1979 film Ogro is also pretty mind-blowing, although minor keys predominate.</resource>
  <resource id="10.129">Algiers has long been hailed as a masterwork and indeed, in my quasi-august opinion, it's absolutely brilliant.</resource>
  <resource id="10.130">(I screened it at VSU in '04 for my faculty advisors and they seemed fairly stunned by it.</resource>
  <resource id="10.131">None of them had ever seen it before.</resource>
  <resource id="10.132">Strange.)</resource>
  <resource id="10.133">Burn!</resource>
  <resource id="10.134">is far more confounding, but its flaws fail to obsure a provocative subtext.</resource>
  <resource id="10.135">Ogro is a Franco-era policier and all the more grim for its muted tone and washed-out palette.</resource>
  <resource id="10.136">Get 'em and watch alongside Click, or New York Minute.</resource>
  <resource id="10.137">Everything will soon become clear.</resource>
</block>

  <resource id="32.520">Broadcasting this time from the New World.</resource>

  <resource id="32.527">I'll start my narrative with a little anecdote about how small the world really is. So I was going to work one day, headed to one of the hostels around Berlin to pick people up for the tour, and then bring them back to the Brandenburg Gate where the tour starts.</resource>
  <resource id="32.528">I had never been to this particular hostel before, and as I entered and gave my little spiel about the tour and asked if anyone wanted to come along, I was met not with a roar of enthusiasm from the hostel residents, but rather a few dull, glass-eyed looks and was generally ignored.</resource>
  <resource id="32.529">As I was preparing to leave, one guy over at the computers yelled out for me to wait, approached me and said hello.</resource>

  <resource id="32.537">It was my first time in this particular theater, hailed as the best theater in Germany.</resource>
  <resource id="32.538">And this particular piece was by far the best play I have EVER seen.</resource>
  <resource id="32.539">It was breathtaking.</resource>

  <resource id="32.547">It was sooooo much fun!</resource>
  <resource id="32.548">I saw most of the major sites on my own, and I went to the theater almost every night I was there.</resource>
  <resource id="32.549">This aspect of the trip was hit and miss. The first piece I saw was a new one by Peter Schaefer called 'Market Boy', directed by Trevor Nunn at the National Theater.</resource>
  <resource id="32.550">I thought: "wow, with that much talent behind it, it's gotta be good!"</resource>
  <resource id="32.551">Wrong.</resource>
  <resource id="32.552">WRONG.</resource>
  <resource id="32.553">Oh how wrong I was.</resource>
  <resource id="32.554">The show was trash.</resource>
  <resource id="32.555">Horrible.</resource>
  <resource id="32.556">Horribly acted, horribly directed, weak script.</resource>
  <resource id="32.557">And interminably long!</resource>
  <resource id="32.558">Three hours of horror.</resource>
  <resource id="32.559">I wanted to leave at intermission, but my date was really enjoying it (should have been a sign) so we stayed.</resource>
  <resource id="32.560">I guess the one good thing I took from it was that I know now that the British are just as capable as we are of producing really awful work.</resource>
  <resource id="32.561">It was nice to understand that, just wish I didn't have to experience it first hand.</resource>
  <resource id="32.562">While in London I also saw Coriolanus at the Globe Theater.</resource>
  <resource id="32.563">Now this was amazing.</resource>
  <resource id="32.564">Shakespeare, old school.</resource>
  <resource id="32.565">The performers were incredible, and one of the generals was played by Geoffrey, the butler from Fresh Prince of Bellaire.</resource>
  <resource id="32.566">That in itself was hilarious, and made us love the show even more.</resource>
  <resource id="32.567">I also saw Les Miserables for the first time, and it was fun.</resource>
  <resource id="32.568">Loved London.</resource>
  <resource id="32.569">The city was great, the energy was incredible.</resource>
  <resource id="32.570">Loved the free museums.</resource>
  <resource id="32.571">Especially the British Museum.</resource>
  <resource id="32.572">Great East London accents.</resource>
  <resource id="32.573">Soooo many monuments, including two statues in front of Buckingham Palace that had a conspicuous Communist message.</resource>
  <resource id="32.574">It was a worker with a hammer and a peasant woman with a sickle situated behind Queen Victoria's back , and glowering at the palace.</resource>
  <resource id="32.575">Now maybe I was just reading too much into this, or maybe I had spent too much time in the eastern bloc talking about Communist symbolism.</resource>
  <resource id="32.576">But I don't' think so.</resource>
  <resource id="32.577">And the funniest part, the two statues said they were donated by New Zealand.</resource>
  <resource id="32.578">Curious.</resource>
  <resource id="32.579">next time you're in Wellington, watch out for the Kiwi Commies!</resource>
  <resource id="32.580">After London came the World Cup.</resource>
  <resource id="32.581">Now I have a confession to make: before the World Cup began I had actually never seen an entire soccer game from start to finish.</resource>
  <resource id="32.582">That changed very quickly.</resource>
  <resource id="32.583">First of all, the atmosphere in the city was insane.</resource>
  <resource id="32.584">There were SO many people from all over the world.</resource>
  <resource id="32.585">They would prowl the city in packs of 6-10, all wearing their national colors, often their flags as capes, drinking absurd amounts of beer and singing their soccer songs.</resource>
  <resource id="32.586">The hordes of people would change from day to day, for instance there was a three day period where we had over 90,000 Swedes in the city, leading up to their dramatic defeat at the hands of Zeh Germans.</resource>
  <resource id="32.587">Then there were the Brazilians, everywhere, singing-clapping-dancing-taking pictures, and of course the pale and pasty Brits with their cock-eyed stares and drinking songs.</resource>
  <resource id="32.588">Loads of fun.</resource>
  <resource id="32.589">I didn't have tickets to any of the games, but my group of friends would always go to a cafe or bar of the nationality that was playing to watch the match.</resource>
  <resource id="32.590">So to a French cafe for the France-Swiss game, a Tapas bar when Spain played, drank Caiprinhas with the passionate Brazilians, etc. It was a colorful and hilarious way to take part without fighting the crowds.</resource>
  <resource id="32.591">And let me tell you how good the World Cup was for Berlin as a city, and Germany as a nation.</resource>
  <resource id="32.592">First of all, Berlin is a hugely under populated city.</resource>
  <resource id="32.593">So it was really nice to have an extra two million people in the city to sort of liven things up.</resource>
  <resource id="32.594">The energy was incredible.</resource>
  <resource id="32.595">Another interesting element was the latent patriotism that emerged during the Cup.</resource>
  <resource id="32.596">Now this merits explaining: before the World Cup began, if you waved a German flag in the streets, you would probably be called a Nazi and at the very least labeled as a radical right wing nutter.</resource>
  <resource id="32.597">You have to understand that the Germans have had a really difficult time with their national identity (and subsequently national pride or patriotism of any kind) since the Second World War.</resource>
  <resource id="32.598">Because of the way Hitler appealed to, and exploited, German nationalism in the 30's and 40's, as a means of supporting his plans of conquest, the Germans are now hyper-sensitive about any kind of patriotic resurgence, and its consequences.</resource>
  <resource id="32.599">Which also explains why they are so openly critical of America's post-9/11 patriotic-fever, in connection with the Bush administrations aggressive foreign policy.</resource>
  <resource id="32.600">And for what it's worth I agree with them, it makes me nervous too.</resource>
  <resource id="32.601">So.</resource>
  <resource id="32.602">taking all of this into account, you can imagine the entire country's surprise when after the Germany-Costa Rica match, in which the Germans soundly defeated them, Germany became one giant street party, shutting down all central streets in the capital with a flood of humanity cheering, yelling, hugging, DRINKING, and curiously.</resource>
  <resource id="32.603">waving German flags everywhere.</resource>
  <resource id="32.604">In the streets, hanging from windows, decorating cars, and painted on faces.</resource>
  <resource id="32.605">People were genuinely excited about supporting their national team, and taking pride in its accomplishments.</resource>
  <resource id="32.606">Now this newfound enthusiasm certainly was not shared by everyone.</resource>
  <resource id="32.607">In fact the entire month of June was one continuous debate in the media between prominent politicians, celebrities, writers (such as Guenther Grass and Christa Wolf), and other intellectuals about the meaning, origin and impact of this nationalism.</resource>
  <resource id="32.608">And of course no one can laboriously dissect an idea better than the Germans.</resource>
  <resource id="32.609">But the general consensus after five weeks of debate was that the newfound national pride was a positive thing.</resource>
  <resource id="32.610">And at that point it was pretty much irrelevant anyway, because after every one of Germany's wins the party was getting bigger and the flag waving more intense.</resource>
  <resource id="32.611">Personally I thought it was wonderful.</resource>
  <resource id="32.612">It was amazing to see my German friends cheering their heads off, enraptured in the glory of a winning team, a team that represents the best of your nation while the whole world watches.</resource>
  <resource id="32.613">It was an important moment for Germany, and in Berlin the atmosphere was pure magic.</resource>
  <resource id="32.614">A side note: the World Cup also went off completely safely.</resource>
  <resource id="32.615">The Berlin police especially handled the situation expertly, and were always present without being obtrusive.</resource>
  <resource id="32.616">In fact they did such a good job, that they are now training the Swiss police for the upcoming European Championship in two years.</resource>
  <resource id="32.617">In June I had also been training at work to lead tours of Sachsenhausen Concentration Camp, which I started at the end of the month.</resource>
  <resource id="32.618">This camp, about 40 miles north of Berlin was the second major camp to be opened in the Nazi system, and was also used by the Russians after the war to intern former Nazi's and anyone else deemed an 'enemy of the state'.</resource>
  <resource id="32.619">Given the dark history of such a place you can well imagine the difficulty of retelling that information, and trying to put it into some kind of historical context.</resource>
  <resource id="32.620">I ended up doing tours out there for the duration of the summer.</resource>
  <resource id="32.621">I found the job challenging, and extremely rewarding.</resource>
  <resource id="32.622">After a July of beautiful weather and great summer adventures, I traveled with my good Australian friend Kurt to a theater festival in Cyprus.</resource>
  <resource id="32.623">The occasion was that a good friend of mine from North Texas, Jessica, was playing the title role in Medea at a theater festival there.</resource>
  <resource id="32.624">I was planning on going to Greece sometime anyway, so we spent three days in Athens, and two and a half in Cyprus.</resource>
  <resource id="32.625">Now this was ridiculous amounts of fun!</resource>
  <resource id="32.626">If Athens had a particular style, it would probably be called: "Landfill-chic".</resource>
  <resource id="32.627">The city is a sweaty, dirty, polluted hole.</resource>
  <resource id="32.628">Take your impression of a squalor-filled Mediterranean capital, and add this to your image: 10 story apartment buildings everywhere, in 100 degree heat, all cooled by wall-mounted air conditioners, a.k.a. swamp boxes.</resource>
  <resource id="32.629">Now take into account that said air conditioners are mounted on the windows, sticking out over the street.</resource>
  <resource id="32.630">Now imagine the condensation from thousands of swamp box coolers pitter pattering on your head as you stroll down the trashy Athens streets.</resource>
  <resource id="32.631">Now add a couple of poorly placed ancient columns.</resource>
  <resource id="32.632">WELCOME TO ATHENS!!</resource>
  <resource id="32.633">Okay, it wasn't really as bad as all that.</resource>
  <resource id="32.634">But it was close.</resource>
  <resource id="32.635">And the city itself really is lovely, in it's own way.</resource>
  <resource id="32.636">But two days back to back was more than enough.</resource>
  <resource id="32.637">We took a day trip out to Sunion, the very tip of Greece, to see the temple of Poseidon.</resource>
  <resource id="32.638">Now this was really amazing.</resource>
  <resource id="32.639">It took us about two hours to get out there, and then we climbed around and played on the rocks for about an hour, taking pics of the magnificent old temple ruin that is literally clinging to the cliff face.</resource>
  <resource id="32.640">And then we watched in amazement with fifty other tourists as the sun went down in front of the temple, over the Mediterranean.</resource>
  <resource id="32.641">Talk about a Bellevue!</resource>
  <resource id="32.642">The sky is so amazing in Greece, it feels like you could really reach up and touch it, it's so close!</resource>
  <resource id="32.643">And of course this just enhanced the magic of the sunset, as the red orb of  the sun ever so slowly retreated behind the furthest hill.</resource>
  <resource id="32.644">So slow in fact we could actually count down it's descent and give it a big round of applause when it winked out of sight.</resource>
  <resource id="32.645">What an experience.</resource>
  <resource id="32.646">So after a few days in Athens, it was off to Cyrprus with Aegean Airlines, which has the best looking flight attendants OF ALL TIME.</resource>
  <resource id="32.647">Just FYI.</resource>
  <resource id="32.648">They also bake bread for you on the plane so that your in flight meal is fresh.</resource>
  <resource id="32.649">Not bad.</resource>
  <resource id="32.650">Cyprus was amazing.</resource>
  <resource id="32.651">We saw Jessica's production of Medea, and were treated incredibly well by her colleagues at the festival.</resource>
  <resource id="32.652">We stayed in a tiny mountain village called Droushia, and these people brought hospitality to incredible new heights.</resource>
  <resource id="32.653">They did everything they could to make us feel welcome, everything from putting us up in a beautiful hotel, refusing to let us pay for anything, and making sure that our stay on their island was as enchanting as the stories always make it out to be.</resource>
  <resource id="32.654">Our first day we went swimming on the south side of the island, in the most brilliant blue water I have ever seen.</resource>
  <resource id="32.655">We spent pretty much the whole day (outside of a decadent lunch at the beach house) in the water.</resource>
  <resource id="32.656">When we bubbled to our hosts that evening about their splendid beaches, they scoffed saying: "The south side, that's not even the good part.</resource>
  <resource id="32.657">Tomorrow we'll go somewhere special."</resource>
  <resource id="32.658">And they weren't kidding.</resource>
  <resource id="32.659">The next morning, the last day of the festival, they piled us all into four wheel drive vehicles, and took us off the map to an area that I will henceforth be labeling The Blue Lagoon.</resource>
  <resource id="32.660">Imagine the most incredible, horseshoe shaped cove, with unimaginably blue water, and a giant stone underneath the water, hundreds of meters long and wide, adding depth to the color.</resource>
  <resource id="32.661">What's more, we had the whole place to ourselves.</resource>
  <resource id="32.662">Our hosts had brought along a lunch of fresh fruit and we broke our fast on a cliff overlooking the whole gorgeous expanse.</resource>
  <resource id="32.663">The Cypriot people are some of the most genuine, hospitable, and quirky people I have ever encountered.</resource>
  <resource id="32.664">It was really interesting staying with them in this little mountain village, and getting to experience a bit of what their culture was all about.</resource>
  <resource id="32.665">Oh and the food is incredible.</resource>
  <resource id="32.666">They make their own very strong coffee, and Haloumi cheese, which we were told (a hundred different times by all the little old ladies) was goat cheese.</resource>
  <resource id="32.667">But not feta.</resource>
  <resource id="32.668">Haloumi.</resource>
  <resource id="32.669">Haloumi has nothing to do with Feta.</resource>
  <resource id="32.670">Don't ever mistake Haloumi for Feta, or you might have a flock of barely intelligible Cypriot grammas spend half an hour pinching your cheek and explaining the difference in broken Greco-English.</resource>
  <resource id="32.671">They take their cheese seriously.</resource>
  <resource id="32.672">So after a few whirlwind days on the island, we flew back to Athens, spent a day seeing the Akropolis, Parthenon, Theater of Dionysos, etc. They all lived up to my expectations, and far exceeded them.</resource>
  <resource id="32.673">Its really a testament to the vision of the ancients that in our world of skyscrapers and technical gadgetry, we are still fascinated by a two thousand year-old arrangement of stones.</resource>
  <resource id="32.674">After returning from Greece my last few weeks in Berlin passed very quickly.</resource>
  <resource id="32.675">I was leading loads of tours, and working on a short film I was directing.</resource>
  <resource id="32.676">The time flew by and before I knew it, August was at an end and my doorstep was graced by my best friend Lance, who had come to Berlin for my last week.</resource>
  <resource id="32.677">So I spent the next six days in a haze of working my final tours, partying, and showing Lance the city that I had come to love so much over the last two years.</resource>
  <resource id="32.678">After a fabulous and emotional going-away party, we left Berlin in the last days of August, and took an ICE express train (sooooo fast) to Zurich.</resource>
  <resource id="32.679">Lance and I spent the better part of the next three days lounging around Zurich and drinking in Switzerland.</resource>
  <resource id="32.680">We were both pretty destroyed from lack of sleep/too much fun over the last week in Berlin, so most of what we did in Zurich consisted of going to the grocery, eating a picnic brunch on the river or the lake, taking a nap after said lunch, and then wandering around the quaint little downtown area and lamenting the fact that we couldn't afford anything.</resource>
  <resource id="32.681">People say London or Paris is expensive; no.</resource>
  <resource id="32.682">Zurich is expensive.</resource>
  <resource id="32.683">But we didn't let that discourage us.</resource>
  <resource id="32.684">We had a ball in Zurich.</resource>
  <resource id="32.685">We went on a walking tour, went to the City Museum (located in an old castle, very cool), and had a good time relaxing and enjoying the AMAZING views.</resource>
  <resource id="32.686">The city is incredibly beautiful, tucked into the Alps and right on the water.</resource>
  <resource id="32.687">Pretty as a picture.</resource>
  <resource id="32.688">After our detox detour en Suisse, we headed via night train to Paris.</resource>
  <resource id="32.689">I think the most important thing I can say about this city, is that all of the rumors: That the city's dirty, the people are rude, the language barrier is insurmountable, the metro is incomprehensible, you things will be stolen, that they hate Americans.</resource>
  <resource id="32.690">are UNTRUE!!</resource>
  <resource id="32.691">There is no city in Europe that I had more trepidation about visiting (because of all the horror stories I had heard) and no city in Europe that it was easier for me to be enchanted by and fall in love with.</resource>
  <resource id="32.692">It's called the city of love and light for a reason.</resource>
  <resource id="32.693">It's so amazingly beautiful, just like all the pictures.</resource>
  <resource id="32.694">The museums are life-changing.</resource>
  <resource id="32.695">The Eiffel Tower is breathtaking, and seeing it in person destroys its cliche and makes you fall in love with the thing.</resource>
  <resource id="32.696">The people are absolutely LOVELY.</resource>
  <resource id="32.697">We encountered so many genuinely wonderful, helpful, content people.</resource>
  <resource id="32.698">Everything from trying to order a baguette in the morning (my Francais is tres terrible) to asking directions or talking to cabbies, we were always pleasantly surprised at how open and welcoming they were.</resource>
  <resource id="32.699">And they all seem to really enjoy their city.</resource>
  <resource id="32.700">They know how beautiful it is, and they appreciate it.</resource>
  <resource id="32.701">And man, it is gorgeous.</resource>
  <resource id="32.702">So many public spaces, parks museums, gardens.</resource>
  <resource id="32.703">And people are out in them all the time.</resource>
  <resource id="32.704">There was a fabulous sense of community there.</resource>
  <resource id="32.705">My favorite part about the city parks were the 'nap chairs'.</resource>
  <resource id="32.706">We had spent four hours in the Louvre, and were totally fried, couldn't see any more art.</resource>
  <resource id="32.707">So we went across the street to the park, it was about 2:00 pm.</resource>
  <resource id="32.708">There were people all over the place, and lots of benches and chairs.</resource>
  <resource id="32.709">We noticed that about half of the chairs were slanted at a 45 degree angle.</resource>
  <resource id="32.710">I thought: "that's strange, what's that about?"</resource>
  <resource id="32.711">We found out when we headed over to two of these chairs that were situated in the shade, and plopped down.</resource>
  <resource id="32.712">They were perfect for taking a catnap.</resource>
  <resource id="32.713">We looked around and noticed that there were lots of people all over the park, in the municipal nap chairs, taking a snooze.</resource>
  <resource id="32.714">Well, when in Rome.</resource>
  <resource id="32.715">so we did the same.</resource>
  <resource id="32.716">Had a little hour sleep, got back up and headed back into the colossal Louvre for another few hours.</resource>
  <resource id="32.717">What other city would use tax payer dollars to provide napping chairs in a public park?</resource>
  <resource id="32.718">Vive la France!</resource>
  <resource id="32.719">We really had a ball in Paris.</resource>
  <resource id="32.720">We went to Versailles, we went to Notre Dame, and on a fabulous bike tour.</resource>
  <resource id="32.721">The most interesting thing we learned on the bike tour was that when the revolutionaries stormed the Bastille, there were only 7 prisoners left in it.</resource>
  <resource id="32.722">6 were petty thieves and the seventh was a boy whose father had sent him there for a few weeks as  disciplinary measure.</resource>
  <resource id="32.723">So the Revloution, and it's over 20,000 beheadings was all for the sake of 'liberating' 7 ne'er-do-wells.</resource>
  <resource id="32.724">Vive la Revolution!</resource>
  <resource id="32.725">Montmartre, the old bohemian artist district, five minutes from the Sacre Coeur Cathedral.</resource>
  <resource id="32.726">Every night after dinner we'd climb the hill to the cathedral and sit on the steps.</resource>
  <resource id="32.727">The Cathedral is the highest point in Paris, and from its northerly vantage point, you have the entire city spread out at your feet.</resource>
  <resource id="32.728">It's a pretty breathtaking sight.</resource>
  <resource id="32.729">On the steps there was always sort of an impromptu party with musicians and performers and other city-watchers gathering with a bottle of wine and a merry spirit.</resource>
  <resource id="32.730">Those evenings on the steps are some of my favorite memories from Paris.</resource>
  <resource id="32.731">So, after five tremendous days, we finally bid adieu to Pair-ee and boarded a plane back to Dallas.</resource>
  <resource id="32.732">Upon arrival back in the states, we changed clothes and went directly to a Counting Crows concert, which was my birthday present from Lance.</resource>
  <resource id="32.733">What an incredible end to an incredible journey.</resource>
  <resource id="32.734">Thus, is my two year Grande Tour at an end.</resource>
  <resource id="32.735">It took me to 11 different countries and incalculable new experiences.</resource>
  <resource id="32.736">I've returned ready for a fresh start, collecting new experiences on this side of the pond.</resource>
  <resource id="32.737">The last few weeks I've been in Lubbock, trying to readjust to life in America and spending lots of time with my family.</resource>
  <resource id="32.738">I was also lucky enough to be hired to direct a show for Frenship high school, which went up last Sunday.</resource>
  <resource id="32.739">It was a Commedia dell'Arte piece titled The Red Hat, and went extremely well.</resource>
  <resource id="32.740">It was a nice way for me to get to stretch those theater muscles and earn a little something at the same time.</resource>
  <resource id="32.741">And though my European adventure is over (for the moment) my American one starts tomorrow.</resource>
  <resource id="32.742">I'm moving to Chicago Sunday morning, in the hopes of getting started there as an director and performer.</resource>
  <resource id="32.743">I'm excited about the move, and the opportunities and challenges that await me there.</resource>
  <resource id="32.744">Well thanks for making it this far!</resource>
  <resource id="32.745">There was  a lot to pack in.</resource>
  <resource id="32.746">I hope all of you are well; that you have made it through the summer, and are excited about the upcoming (rapidly approaching) holiday season!</resource>
  <resource id="32.747">I'll be in New York for New Year's, so if you're going to be in that area, let me know and we'll ring it in together.</resource>
  <resource id="32.748">I wish you all a fabulous week, and a warm and safe start to the wintry season.</resource>
  <resource id="32.749">All the best till we meet again!</resource>
</block>

<block id="block 62">
  <resource id="62.161">Our emotions are a gift from God.</resource>
  <resource id="62.162">When emotions are allowed to work as they are intended to work they can bless us and others in many ways.</resource>
  <resource id="62.163">But in order to recieve the blessing we must first realize that our emotions are always connected to a need being met or unmet.</resource>
  <resource id="62.164">By listening to our emotions and asking ourselves why we are feeling a particular emotion we can discover an unmet need or a need that has been satisfied.</resource>
  <resource id="62.165">For instance, if I feel sad because I have not seen my family in a long time my need for connection has not been met.</resource>
  <resource id="62.166">On the otherhand, if I feel happy because I have spent time with my family then my need for connection has been met.</resource>
  <resource id="62.167">Yet, many times we do not listen to our emotions and completely ignore them and/or act violently towards others as a result of not looking at the need we have.</resource>
  <resource id="62.168">How many times have we felt angry and as a result insulted, judged, criticized, blamed or physically hurt someone?</resource>
  <resource id="62.169">Probably too many to count.</resource>
  <resource id="62.170">Many of us don't know what to do when we feel angry, furious, frustrated, hopeless, lonely, despondent and many other emotions because no one has taught us what to do.</resource>
  <resource id="62.171">We live in a society that has encourages us to stuff our emotions and a society that is by the most part feelings illiterate.</resource>
  <resource id="62.172">So, what can we do?</resource>
  <resource id="62.173">Well, for starters become aware of what we are feeling.</resource>
  <resource id="62.174">When we are upset we can simply ask ourselves, "What am I feeling?"</resource>
  <resource id="62.175">We can become conscious of what is going on inside us.</resource>
  <resource id="62.176">Once we put our finger on the emotion we are feeling we can then go on to vocalize it.</resource>
  <resource id="62.177">Marshall Rosenberg, author of Nonviolent Communication, has a four-step process for communication that incorporates identifying our feelings and needs into the model.</resource>
  <resource id="62.178">Part of that model involves asking the question, "I am feeling _______ because I am needing ______."</resource>
  <resource id="62.179">So, what we can do when we are feeling upset is ask ourselves, "What am I feeling and needing right now?" or say, "I am feeling _______ because I am needing __________."</resource>
  <resource id="62.180">Rosenberg also calls this four step process "giraffe language."</resource>
  <resource id="62.181">He coined it giraffe language because the giraffe has the largest heart of all the mammals.</resource>
  <resource id="62.182">The heart is about 3 feet long and 3 inches thick all around.</resource>
  <resource id="62.183">The idea is that Nonviolent communication, which focuses on feelings and needs is the language of the heart because it focuses on what is alive in the heart.</resource>
  <resource id="62.184">When we focus on our feelings and needs we also avoid focusing on what others are saying or doing that is bothering us.</resource>
  <resource id="62.185">By stating our feelings and needs to others we avoid blaming and criticizing them.</resource>
  <resource id="62.186">We can then go on to making a request instead of a demand to help ourselves satisfy our now recognized need.</resource>
  <resource id="62.187">When others are angry or upset with us we can also use this technique on them.</resource>
  <resource id="62.188">We can ask them, "Are you feeling ______ because you are needing _______?"</resource>
  <resource id="62.189">They will appreciate that we are focusing on them and let us know if we are right.</resource>
  <resource id="62.190">If we are not correct they will correct us and we will be closer to identifying the need they have and finding a way to resolve the situation.</resource>
  <resource id="62.191">By satisfying our needs and the needs of others we can reach a fair negotiation.</resource>
  <resource id="62.192">Another thing that we can do is become aware of certain feelings that have had a tendency in the past to trigger negative actions toward ourselves and others.</resource>
  <resource id="62.193">We can make a list of these feelings and be on the look out for them.</resource>
  <resource id="62.194">It may be that we need to take a timeout when we feel these feelings to avoid a conflict.</resource>
  <resource id="62.195">For instance, if we feel furious it may be better to take a one hour timeout and calm down.</resource>
  <resource id="62.196">Then when we are calm, focus on sharing our feelings and needs with others.</resource>
  <resource id="62.197">Many times in the past we have reacted like a lion when we feel angry or frustrated with others or ourselves.</resource>
  <resource id="62.198">Taking time to focus on our feelings and needs helps us not let the lion out and speak from the heart that language of the giraffe with ourselves and others.</resource>
  <resource id="62.199">For more information on Nonviolent Communication click here.</resource>
</block>

<block id="block 69">
  <resource id="69.380">First of all, I would like to say that I am proud of myself.</resource>
  <resource id="69.381">What I completed in one day today, was not possible to me last week.</resource>
  <resource id="69.382">Today, I can say I actually taught for all three of my periods.</resource>
  <resource id="69.383">Here is a run down of my overhwhelming, but wonderful day:</resource>
  <resource id="69.384">I walked into my first period only to find a sub.</resource>
  <resource id="69.385">I decided then and there that I would take advantage of the situation and teach the class.</resource>
  <resource id="69.386">I asked the sub if he did not mind my taking over, and of course he complied.</resource>
  <resource id="69.387">I figured I knew the material more than he did, and more importantly I knew the students and how the class should run.</resource>
  <resource id="69.388">So the bell rang, the sub introduced himself and I took over.</resource>
  <resource id="69.389">I had them write in their journals for a few minutes, then proceeded to facilitate the day's assignments.</resource>
  <resource id="69.390">I allowed them 10-15 minutes to finish up the work from yesterday before we began a new piece of literature.</resource>
  <resource id="69.391">After they turned that in, I introduced "Poor Richard's Almanac," which we read as a class.</resource>
  <resource id="69.392">This was a very short reading so I gave the class an assignment to go along with the reading.</resource>
  <resource id="69.393">They had the remainder of the class to complete the assignment while I went around to monitor their work and answer questions.</resource>
  <resource id="69.394">The period ran smoothly under my direction most suprisingly to me. I actually liked being thrown into the situation without having prior knowledge.</resource>
  <resource id="69.395">On the other hand, I did know that today I was to teach a full period lesson to my freshmen for periods 3 &amp; 6.</resource>
  <resource id="69.396">For today's lesson I taught them how to determine the mood in a piece of literature based on the definition, an example, and a story out of their literature books.</resource>
  <resource id="69.397">I began my lesson by explaining what the term "mood" meant.</resource>
  <resource id="69.398">I explained to them that the "mood" of the story could be determined based on the word choice used by the author.</resource>
  <resource id="69.399">To give them a better understanding of how "mood" works, I presented them with a paragraph that I wrote on the overhead.</resource>
  <resource id="69.400">I had them follow along as I read out loud.</resource>
  <resource id="69.401">After I was done reading my paragraph, I had the students tell me what was the mood I had created.</resource>
  <resource id="69.402">Almost everyone participated, and for every type of mood they came up with, I had them point out places in the paragraph that helped them to determine the mood.</resource>
  <resource id="69.403">My example worked well.</resource>
  <resource id="69.404">That was a segway into today's story.</resource>
  <resource id="69.405">Today we read, "The Talk" by Gary Soto.</resource>
  <resource id="69.406">I gave a brief background on the author and the story before we began.</resource>
  <resource id="69.407">I read the first paragraph, then I drew names out of my little tin container to complete the reading.</resource>
  <resource id="69.408">After certain spots, I would have them go over plot summary just to make sure they were following along.</resource>
  <resource id="69.409">At the end of the story, I had them do the same thing they did with my paragraph to determine the mood.</resource>
  <resource id="69.410">After a discussion, I asked them to take out a piece of paper.</resource>
  <resource id="69.411">I had them answer a question along the lines of, "how would the mood of the story have been different if Soto wrote this as a child?"</resource>
  <resource id="69.412">They answered on their papers, then shared out as a class.</resource>
  <resource id="69.413">This completed my very first lesson for periods 3 &amp; 6.</resource>
</block>

<block id="block 119">
  <resource id="119.500">A few days ago, I ran into this article online: http://msnbc.msn.com/id/14126192/ talking about statistics regarding the number of friends people having being at some sort of new low.</resource>
  <resource id="119.501">Not long afterwards, I had a conversation with Colin that crossed some of the same subject on a more personal basis, and it's had me thinking a lot about friends and friendships and my experiences with both.</resource>
  <resource id="119.502">I guess I fit the group the article refers to, in regards to having only 2 or 3 friends that would fall into the "support mechanism" category, if only because of circumstantial or geographical factors being taken into consideration as well.</resource>
  <resource id="119.503">One of those 3 will be moving this fall, and the others have an awful lot going on in their lives, in addition to being less available than the first by a long shot anyhow.</resource>
  <resource id="119.504">Realistically, when friend number one exits the scene, my options for anything passing for routine entertainment or companionship pretty much vanish until I can cultivate new ones.</resource>
  <resource id="119.505">It's both a frightening and interesting prospect.</resource>
  <resource id="119.506">On a larger scale, this really makes me think about the way that I've cultivated my friendships, and the way I've maintained them as well.</resource>
  <resource id="119.507">I definitely feel like, moreso than any other time in my life, I'm operating almost at the ground floor in regards to the friendships I have and the way I interact with those friends.</resource>
  <resource id="119.508">I've burned a lot of bridges and engaged in decisions which have hampered my ability to be trusted as much as I'd like to be by some of my friends, and likewise, there have been things which have altered my ability to fully engage some people as well, be they circumstacial or environmental or whatever.</resource>
  <resource id="119.509">I'm not going to name names or point the finger, as this is much more a reflection of myself than any sort of statement or accusation of anyone or any event.</resource>
  <resource id="119.510">Suffice to say that there are a fair amount of people out there whom I would count among my friends, but limited few whom I would count as good friends, due to actions both inside and outside of my role in that friendship.</resource>
  <resource id="119.511">A lot of it is just the simple matter of neglect, the missed opportunities, unreturned calls, and shifting of priorities that causes a full-time friendship to fall into part-time status.</resource>
  <resource id="119.512">We're getting older, we have a lot more work-related matters and our personal lives can get pretty complicated.</resource>
  <resource id="119.513">You build friendships as fortifications against getting too old too fast, but sometimes life just catches up with you regardless of your efforts.</resource>
  <resource id="119.514">On a "big picture" scale, I'd like to believe that I still hold the same value system that I held when I was 18 years old, now a scary half-a-lifetime ago.</resource>
  <resource id="119.515">If I broke things down on a value-by-value basis, I'd immediately begin to see the compromises, both self-imposed and accidentally stumbled-upon, and it paints a more honest picture of a lifetime of expectations tempered by both the hand of fate and the workings of both moments of incredible strength and incredible weakness.</resource>
  <resource id="119.516">All in all, I'm satisfied with the bigger picture and angry as fuck at the smaller details, but that's what keeps you going, isn't it?</resource>
  <resource id="119.517">If I didn't have the desire to be better than what I currently am, would I have impetus to keep living at all?</resource>
  <resource id="119.518">I hope not.</resource>
  <resource id="119.519">As far as "support mechanisms" go, I've seen what they do to people- at their best, they act as a bridge to help people reach things both within and outside themself that they may not have been previously able to reach.</resource>
  <resource id="119.520">And at their worst, they act as a crutch, keeping you mired in the same situations you've been trying to remove yourself from, were it not for the comfort and familiarity you've found yourself unwilling to potentially sacrifice to actually find yourself in a better place.</resource>
  <resource id="119.521">Home may be where you hang your hat, but happiness is sometimes found far from home or in the last place you'd even expect to look for it.</resource>
  <resource id="119.522">I think sometimes it can be easy to lose track of the idea that happiness and comfort are two completely separate entities and having one does not guarantee or equal having the other (some of the time, at least.</resource>
  <resource id="119.523">It goes back to that whole journey vs. destination idea, in that some people only mark success by arriving at a goal, while others are capable of enjoying the efforts required to reach that goal as well.</resource>
  <resource id="119.524">In the end, who is more satisfied- the person who suffers and bitches because things aren't perfect all the way until the very last detail has been covered, or the person who is able to take pleasure from the process every step of the way?</resource>
  <resource id="119.525">if the goals are similar, which one would you prefer to be?</resource>
  <resource id="119.526">Now which one are you most of the time?</resource>
  <resource id="119.527">Friendship works the same way- some people only appreciate friendships which are productive and goal-oriented, whereas there are also friendships that meander along the way, and each moment can be as interesting and rewarding as the ones before and after.</resource>
  <resource id="119.528">This works in reverse as well, with more negative effects and outcomes, obviously, any you seldom realize it until it's too late.</resource>
  <resource id="119.529">It can be really difficult to appreciate just what a friendship really means to you, especially when it's something you can take for granted fairly easily if you aren't careful.</resource>
  <resource id="119.530">I know I'm guilty of that all the time.</resource>
  <resource id="119.531">I found a really honest adage that goes like this: The past is gone, the future's unknown, but today's a gift- that's why they call it "the present"- it may seem like a silly sentiment, but it makes a lot of sense.</resource>
  <resource id="119.532">We take a lot of shit for granted, and we can get really caught up in little problems that seem inescapable, moments come and go, and our actions and inactions towards the people closest to us will either draw them closer to us or push them away.</resource>
  <resource id="119.533">Often we fail to realize what we've lost until something becomes too distant and difficult to regain.</resource>
  <resource id="119.534">Old wounds fester into something that can't be healed, neglect becomes forgetfulness which then becomes amnesia, and before long you wonder if it ever happened.</resource>
  <resource id="119.535">It sucks, and it takes effort to keep a swerving friendship from heading down these sort of paths, but the effort far outweighs the consequences of failing to do so.</resource>
  <resource id="119.536">So what does this mean to me then?</resource>
  <resource id="119.537">Fuck if I know.</resource>
  <resource id="119.538">It means I have some tough choices to make.</resource>
  <resource id="119.539">I can only change my own mind, so whatever efforts I expend have to be met with some sort of equal efforts or the process goes nowhere and frustrates me the entire way.</resource>
  <resource id="119.540">Is it worth it, or is it better to just let fate play out its' hand and look for a new deal?</resource>
  <resource id="119.541">There's usually no way to know until it's too late regardless.</resource>
  <resource id="119.542">I've come this far with less insight, so I guess I'll make it to wherever I'm going one way or another, but it's nice to think it won't always be a long, lonely road.</resource>
  <resource id="119.543">I guess I do what I can to make the most of what I have, if it's not enough then it likely never was, and in that case, at least I know I tried.</resource>
  <resource id="119.544">If being a friend isn't about trying, then I've been taking the wrong approach all along.</resource>
</block>

<block id="block 125">
  <resource id="125.639">As we wrote in a recent posting, even in a favorable political climate Democratic candidates face a significant challenge in turning out their base voters.</resource>
  <resource id="125.640">Despite widespread dissatisfaction among Democrats with the Bush administration and overall Republican governance, we detect no real signs so far (at least in Harris County) of a groundswell to actually vote in November.</resource>
  <resource id="125.641">An oft-repeated notion in recent weeks, however, has been that this year Republicans too will have a difficult time turning out their party faithful on November 7th.</resource>
  <resource id="125.642">The argument goes that anger nationally over rising gas prices and health care costs, a protracted, expensive war with no conclusion in sight, and a perception among religious conservatives that George W. Bush has failed to deliver on the social issues that motivate them has taken a toll with GOP base voters.</resource>
  <resource id="125.643">Here in Texas we add unhappiness with Governor Rick Perry and the Republican-controlled State Legislature over the business tax and failure to pass property tax appraisal caps to the mix, as well as the coming campaign ugliness between Governor Good Hair and rival Carole Keeton Strayhorn, and we have a recipe for depressed GOP turnout in November.</resource>
  <resource id="125.644">Well, maybe.</resource>
  <resource id="125.645">As noted in the latest issue of US News &amp; World Report, new polling has Republican insiders feeling pretty darn good about turning out their folks this fall, with confidence that they will vote to return their party's elected officials to power.</resource>
  <resource id="125.646">The article cites a recent strategic memo to national GOP Party Chair Ken Mehlman from Market Strategies, which highlights polling (granted, six-week old polling) showing the base is in line and ready to hit the polls.</resource>
  <resource id="125.647">Is this spin or is it true?</resource>
  <resource id="125.648">Obviously no way to tell at this point, but any Democratic victory strategy that assumes weak Republican turnout as a key to winning is folly.</resource>
  <resource id="125.649">Democrats should count on having to win in spite of solid GOP voter performance -- any dropoff will be gravy.</resource>
  <resource id="125.650">To be safe, just remember that old joke about the Republican voter who drives past his polling place once a week, just to make sure there's not an election going on.</resource>
  <resource id="125.651">Except it's no joke.</resource>
  <resource id="125.652">In other words, the mantra for Democratic candidates this political season: persuade everyone you can, turn out your base, and assume all the other guy's usual folks will vote.</resource>
  <resource id="125.653">In other words Democrats, work like hell until November 7th.</resource>
</block>

  <resource id="141.19">While its dance was beautiful, in a way.</resource>
  <resource id="141.20">it was not self-determined.</resource>
  <resource id="141.21">I want the next 40 years to be lived on purpose.</resource>
  <resource id="141.22">How do I do that?</resource>
  <resource id="141.23">And how can I start now?</resource>
  <resource id="141.24">One of my most surprising observations about this whole ritual thing is how much easier it was to establish new rituals when I was on location, as opposed to being at home.</resource>
  <resource id="141.25">And, the corollary: how easy it is to slip into old habits, once back at home.</resource>
  <resource id="141.26">[It makes sense that there is concern for addicts or alcoholics who successfully gets straight or sober some place other than where they live.</resource>
  <resource id="141.27">and then eventually have to return home to old triggers.]</resource>
  <resource id="141.28">So anyway.</resource>
  <resource id="141.29">Ritual.</resource>
  <resource id="141.30">Rut.</resource>
  <resource id="141.31">I guess the difference is twofold: rituals are performed by choice and are psychologically, emotionally, or physically beneficial, whereas ruts, on the other hand, are not executed so much on purpose, as they somehow come about mostly by default.</resource>
  <resource id="141.32">That, and they may not be exactly great for you.</resource>
  <resource id="141.33">I think ritual is important because it lends predictability, structure, and things to look forward to - to our otherwise harried, demanding, and random lives.</resource>
  <resource id="141.34">I'm not advocating all-structure, all-the-time or anything.</resource>
  <resource id="141.35">But I do know the perils of a life devoid of ritual.</resource>
  <resource id="141.36">and it is fraught with procrastination and rut.</resource>
  <resource id="141.37">At least in my experience.</resource>
  <resource id="141.38">I'd like to say that my rituals are more active and crunchy.</resource>
  <resource id="141.39">but alas, they aren't (yet).</resource>
  <resource id="141.40">Here are some rituals I HAVE managed to cultivate:</resource>
  <resource id="141.41">* Every day when I come home from work, the first thing I do is put on my pajamas.</resource>
  <resource id="141.42">Maybe that doesn't sound like a ritual.</resource>
  <resource id="141.43">but when I started thinking about things I do regularly that I enjoy, that's definitely one of them.</resource>
  <resource id="141.44">* Every other Saturday, Catharine and I get manicures and pedicures.</resource>
  <resource id="141.45">A time to relax, be pampered, catch up.</resource>
  <resource id="141.46">and we come away with the cutest toes ever!</resource>
  <resource id="141.47">* A ritual I absolutely adore is going to the outdoor screenings at the Hollywood Forever Cemetery during the summer.</resource>
  <resource id="141.48">There are all sorts of mini-rituals that go into this regular event, including the collection of picnic dinner items and the precision methodology by which one gets the perfect spot on the lawn.</resource>
  <resource id="141.49">* When I'm NOT working, I have been known to take myself to brunch on the weekends: just me and whatever novel I'm currently enjoying.</resource>
  <resource id="141.50">* Gardening used to be a cherished ritual of mine, but now my house just looks barren and abandoned.</resource>
  <resource id="141.51">Maybe after this show ends, I'll be able to start a new garden.</resource>
  <resource id="141.52">I've just realized in trying to make a list of my rituals, that I really don't have many.</resource>
  <resource id="141.53">How very telling.</resource>
  <resource id="141.54">This is not good.</resource>
  <resource id="141.55">When I imagine the rituals of others, things like a daily, morning run (something hard for me to fathom, I admit), meditation, feeding the ducks on Thursdays, after-dinner strolls with your family, poker night, date night.</resource>
  <resource id="141.56">and the like are what come to mind.</resource>
  <resource id="141.57">My list of ruts is long and embarrassing and includes things like:</resource>
  <resource id="141.58">* I'm happy to taste any new flavor of ice cream (I love to try new flavors!), but when it comes to ordering, it's always Mint Chip.</resource>
  <resource id="141.59">Always.</resource>
  <resource id="141.60">and.</resource>
  <resource id="141.61">* My fear of insomnia keeps me from going to bed when I'm really tired.</resource>
  <resource id="141.62">Is that a rut.</resource>
  <resource id="141.63">or just something totally mental that I do?</resource>
  <resource id="141.64">Anyway, I don't want to sully this post with MY list of rituals and ruts.</resource>
  <resource id="141.65">I'd like to hear about some of the rituals that you hold dear and why YOU think ritual is important to your life.</resource>
  <resource id="141.66">How do you think we can instill more meaningful rituals into our lives?</resource>
</block>

<block id="block 142">
  <resource id="142.67">"The image of cartoonist after cartoonist sitting at folding tables selling comic books is truly a pathetic one.</resource>
  <resource id="142.68">From talking with cartoonists, I get the sense that most of them find it debilitating, too.</resource>
  <resource id="142.69">It's hard, often dreary work.</resource>
  <resource id="142.70">It can be ego-crushing when no one comes to buy anything.</resource>
  <resource id="142.71">I would hate to do it myself."</resource>
  <resource id="142.72">- Bart Beaty, whom I had the pleasure of meeting at the show, from his Conversational Eurocomics column on what SPX could learn from some European conventions</resource>
  <resource id="142.73">I really enjoyed myself at SPX, that delightsome Bethesda megamall of rare and exclusive purchases and the men and women who brought the wares about.</resource>
  <resource id="142.74">Don't sweat it, that's just my perception: I traveled the hour and a half trip down on Saturday with American Hero Chris Mautner (he attained that storied status by agreeing to drive), arrived a half-hour after the doors opened, stayed until 5:30, then went right back home.</resource>
  <resource id="142.75">That means I didn't see the Ignatz Awards, didn't partake of any post-show gatherings, and missed a big chunk of panels.</resource>
  <resource id="142.76">My SPX, in other words, was full of shopping and stalking the aisles.</resource>
  <resource id="142.77">Commerce!</resource>
  <resource id="142.78">Show debuts!</resource>
  <resource id="142.79">I met an awful lot of fine creators, but I hated taking up much of their table time; heaven knows by the afternoon there's a swarm of people waiting to trudge through the byways between tables, and one thousand distractions to attend to.</resource>
  <resource id="142.80">I'd never been to a comics show in my life, so I'd not known any SPX other than what was presented to me there: a pretty swank hotel, a table to pay at in the hall outside, and big ballroom with everyone selling everything inside, and a bunch of other rooms to have educational and informative events in, though all of those were down the hall, around the bend, and down an escalator.</resource>
  <resource id="142.81">There were no signs to direct you to the panels or whatnot, though it wasn't hard to find someone to ask direction of.</resource>
  <resource id="142.82">Still, the separation-by-floor carried a certain symbolism for me when coupled with the atmosphere; upstairs was the carnival of monies and tables, downstairs were the talks and trophies.</resource>
  <resource id="142.83">Upstairs there were masses of shoppers with bags and backpacks and totes, cookies and candies and muffins on certain tables to attract attention, and even a pair of folks operating hand puppets to entice a stray passerby.</resource>
  <resource id="142.84">Downstairs there were hushed people crouched before open doors, the portals leading to capacity talks.</resource>
  <resource id="142.85">There was some sort of bizarre crystal bridge hanging by strings above the escalator, and it certainly did feel like you were crossing into a different, segmented zone of the convention.</resource>
  <resource id="142.86">All of it in a building off the highway, malls and fences and the whipping metal sea of vehicles all around it.</resource>
  <resource id="142.87">To say there was no color at all would be misleading, since it was a nice day and there were a number of trees and things, but I did not detect any local pulse at all.</resource>
  <resource id="142.88">It was a hotel, on a road.</resource>
  <resource id="142.89">I went out for lunch with Chris and Gina Gagliano of First Second; food became curiously hard to find within hiking distance (in the interests of full disclosure, my mastery of direction also had us walking the wrong way for a good while).</resource>
  <resource id="142.90">After we passed by a large parking lot promising a furniture blowout sale, as well as a haunted attraction and possibly ponies or something in the back, we decided to turn around and stop at a little diner we'd passed on the way.</resource>
  <resource id="142.91">It was decent food, Gina got us very enthused about some of First Second's upcoming releases involving Eddie Campbell, Gipi, and the ever-prolific Joann Sfar, Chris blinked a lot in the direction of his sandwich since he hadn't been to sleep since October 2nd, and I told Gina the secret of why there are no pictures on this site (I was bullied by a JPEG in high school).</resource>
  <resource id="142.92">But we didn't hang around.</resource>
  <resource id="142.93">We hurried back from the gray highway to the center of comics and people, followed close behind by haunted ponies who'd just bought cheap Barcaloungers for their ghost stable and were now jonesing for independent comics.</resource>
  <resource id="142.94">Oh, but don't presume that I'm somehow upset at the purchase-happy nature of the show's afternoon - far from it!</resource>
  <resource id="142.95">I love walking around for things to buy, and traveling less than two hours to arrive at the comics spread of my dreams won't raise too many personal hackles, let me tell you.</resource>
  <resource id="142.96">I also made it a point to say hello to everyone there with whom I'd ever communicated on the internet, though I couldn't find anyone matching Johanna Draper Carlson's description and I totally missed Douglas Wolk.</resource>
  <resource id="142.97">But I saw a lot of good people, and finally put some faces to names.</resource>
  <resource id="142.98">I'm also really glad I had Chris with me, since he knew a lot of faces that I didn't; he made a fine guide, though one of us would occasionally wander away from the other in the vein of the old NES version of Who Framed Roger Rabbit?, though I'm unsure as to which one of us was Bob Hoskins.</resource>
  <resource id="142.99">Actually, I think maybe the best thing about SPX is how everyone is kind of pressed together, comics placed near one another seemingly without much concern for subject matter coherency (I heard it was a bit different in prior years, with multiple selling rooms and all); if the creators in attendance and the books on display weren't a comprehensive picture of English-language non-superhero comics, there was at least enough diversity to manage the illusion of comprehensiveness.</resource>
  <resource id="142.100">A diverse audience too, everything from young to old, from old-school nerds to stylish-looking folk, from aged hippies to teenage Goths.</resource>
  <resource id="142.101">To look only at this crowd, you'd imagine that there was no question as to the diversity of the comics-reading audience.</resource>
  <resource id="142.102">I went to two panels, Scott McCloud's and Brian Chippendale's.</resource>
  <resource id="142.103">There was nowhere to sit in the former, since I got there late, so I sort of drifted in and out of hearing distance.</resource>
  <resource id="142.104">At one point, McCloud began vigorously drawing a chart to illustrate different types of artists, all the while telling stories of the message board clashes between forces on places like the Comics Journal board.</resource>
  <resource id="142.105">Someone behind me turned to someone else and whispered "It's just like Civil War."</resource>
  <resource id="142.106">Afterward (a solid half-hour afterward, I believe), McCloud could be seen out in the hallway, taking questions from anyone who happened to want to talk to him away from the bustle of his table upstairs.</resource>
  <resource id="142.107">The Chippendale talk was different, a highly entertaining, digression-prone ramble by the artist through his history and procedure, moderated by Dan Nadel and with extra bonus contributions from Brian Ralph, who was seated in the back of the room.</resource>
  <resource id="142.108">Nobody was particularly brusque at SPX, at least from among the artists, but I found Chippendale to be consistently the most emphatic and friendly creator around.</resource>
  <resource id="142.109">And I bought a good deal of stuff too.</resource>
  <resource id="142.110">I tried to follow Tom's rule of not buying anything on my first trip around the room, but that didn't quite hold.</resource>
  <resource id="142.111">I managed to get through the whole show without finding anything I really wanted sold out, however, and I only had to stand in the Line of Shame at the ATM once.</resource>
  <resource id="142.112">BOOKS I GOT:</resource>
  <resource id="142.113">Abraxas and the Earthman by Rick Veitch (debuted at the show, a collection of the Epic Illustrated serial; an additional collection of Veitch's short comics from the same venue is coming soon)</resource>
  <resource id="142.114">Awakening Comics #1-2 by Steve Peters &amp; friends (I reviewed his The Origin of Sparky a few weeks ago; having walked away from the table, and indeed the entire convention, I suddenly realized that I'd forgotten to actually tell Steve Peters who I was - another triumph of intellect!)</resource>
  <resource id="142.115">Cinema Sewer #19 edited by Robin Bougie (new issue of the funny, comics-heavy pamphlet-format magazine devoted entirely to the finest in exploitation and pornographic film - a creator's personality-powered publication, and I assure you that Robin Bougie is in real life exactly the way he portrays himself in the mag)</resource>
  <resource id="142.116">Cold Heat #1-2 (of 12) by Ben Jones &amp; Frank Santoro (#2 debuted at the show; the odd, semi-abstract fantasy/action book that Diamond initially disliked a while back, complaining of its format despite the fact that it's 100% a serialized pamphlet-format comic)</resource>
  <resource id="142.117">Comics Comics #1-2 edited by Timothy Hodler &amp; Dan Nadel (I mentioned this yesterday, but didn't note that I also got a hard copy of issue #1)</resource>
  <resource id="142.118">Dave K. Greatest Hits 1999-2004 by Dave Kiersh (snapped up from the Bodega Distribution table, a collection of work from a prominent minicomics fellow; Kiersh was actually sitting right in front of my the whole time and I had no idea who he was, and when the guy I was paying mentioned that "Dave" was here to see if I wanted it signed, I kind of stared at him dully before getting what was going on - I also totally forgot to buy Leif Goldberg's National Waste #6 and say hi to Heidi MacDonald who was standing next to me at the table a bit earlier, so it was a real hat trick of inattentive triumph)</resource>
  <resource id="142.119">Ed the Happy Clown #2 (of 9) by Chester Brown (the one I was missing - who says you can't score back-issues at these things?!)</resource>
  <resource id="142.120">Fuzz &amp; Pluck in Splitsville #4 (of 5) by Ted Stearn (surely I can't be the only fan of the mighty Fuzz &amp; Pluck; still, I've never seen this in a store, though Fantagraphics had it at their table)</resource>
  <resource id="142.121">House of Twelve, at the movies Vol.</resource>
  <resource id="142.122">2 #1: Heavy Metal by various (from House of Twelve, a free giveaway show debut, parodying the original movie version of the magazine)</resource>
  <resource id="142.123">Krayons Ego by various (very nice 2006 minicomic anthology spoofing Kramers Ergot, though it's most highly reminiscent of Kurt Wolfgang's old Lo-Jinx; a semi-heated, semi-affectionate lampoon of various contemporary independent comics names, Paper Rad or members thereof apparently the most popular target, though the Jeffrey Brown stuff is maybe the nastiest - Chris really liked it when I showed it to him, but then it was all sold out when he went back for a copy of his own)</resource>
  <resource id="142.124">New Tales of Old Palomar #1 (of 3) by Gilbert Hernandez (new miniseries of stand-alone tales, presented in the Ignatz format, debuting at a show featuring the Ignatz Awards; I remember when this was first released I heard it was going to be titled simply 'New Palomar' like it was New X-Men)</resource>
  <resource id="142.125">Ninja by Brian Chippendale (debuted at the show, probably the book of the show, and I've gushed enough already; when I went to Rick Veitch's table, he became momentarily transfixed with the transfixed and murmured "I want to make a book like this!")</resource>
  <resource id="142.126">Paping #11 by various (older issue of a deluxe anthology by some interesting-looking creators; the real showpiece was issue #14, with its solid wood covers and impressive prints 'n comics mishmash interior, the whole thing bound by red shoelaces; I just didn't have the roughly $30 needed to spend on it by that point in the show, though)</resource>
  <resource id="142.127">Project: Romantic by various (debuted at the show, the newest in AdHouse's Project series of anthologies, this one strictly devoted to romance comics)</resource>
  <resource id="142.128">Pulphope.A by Paul Pope (a show exclusive, I think; not the full Pope art book coming soon from AdHouse, but a 200 copy ashcan with some exclusive stuff)</resource>
  <resource id="142.129">R.</resource>
  <resource id="142.130">Kelly's "Trapped in the Closet" Starring Batman (Abridged) by K. Thor Jensen (a reenactment of the continuing American epic, with members of the Batman cast playing each role)</resource>
  <resource id="142.131">Sermons #2 by Kevin Huizenga (found at the Buenaventura Press table, debuted at the show I think; another installment of Huizenga's interesting sketchbook minicomic concept, dedicated wholly to drawings made and notes taken during church services - it's "KH Book #9" in Huizenga's numbering scheme, if you're keeping track)</resource>
  <resource id="142.132">Service Industry by t. edward bak (much-recommended and indeed very impressive 2005 minicomic by bak, who's set to appear in Fantagraphics' MOME at some point in the future; a potent mix of autobiography, fantasy, subconscious yearning, and personal philosophy, all in 28 b&amp;w and color pages)</resource>
  <resource id="142.133">Shadowland by Kim Deitch (the big new Fantagraphics collection of some of my personal favorite underground-era cartoonist's less-seen works, all of it concerning the affairs of an ill-fated family; debuted at the show, and I bought the last copy, so if you couldn't find it you might as well blame me)</resource>
  <resource id="142.134">So it was a nice trip.</resource>
  <resource id="142.135">A lot of good things to have, and good hands to shake.</resource>
  <resource id="142.136">I hope to do it again, and add on a few more convention experience points.</resource>
  <resource id="142.137">I'm not leveled up yet, no sir.</resource>

<block id="block 143">
  <resource id="143.138">My Mother Is A Fish</resource>
  <resource id="143.139">Do you know some tiger sharks sleep?</resource>
  <resource id="143.140">I think it's in the Gulf of Mexico or the Gulf of California, I can't remember which, but there's so much oxygen in the water they actually sleep.</resource>
  <resource id="143.141">I don't sleep.</resource>
  <resource id="143.142">No, it's alright, I've had a lot of time to come to terms with this.</resource>
  <resource id="143.143">Some of my girlfriends have found it a little strange; I think it's one of the reasons they break up with me in the end, as if they need anymore reasons.</resource>
  <resource id="143.144">I just lay in the bed next to them while they snore, or they breathe softly, or they dream, or they do whatever it is sleeping people do.</resource>
  <resource id="143.145">Most of the time, I sit in a chair.</resource>
  <resource id="143.146">I own a bed because it would be weird if I didn't own a bed.</resource>
  <resource id="143.147">People would come over and say, hey, Angel, you don't own a bed, you must be some kind of freak.</resource>
  <resource id="143.148">Because I can't just have a bedroom with bookshelves and dressers, or everyone would know something was wrong with me. And there is something wrong with me, but I just can't figure it out.</resource>
  <resource id="143.149">To try and remedy this, I spend my nights reading.</resource>
  <resource id="143.150">I just finished a book about the importance of dreams.</resource>
  <resource id="143.151">How important can they be?</resource>
  <resource id="143.152">I don't have them and I'm still here.</resource>
  <resource id="143.153">But it was a good read, and if I did have a dream where I had sex with my mother, well, now I'd know what it means.</resource>
  <resource id="143.154">Sometimes, I can't reading about this condition, though.</resource>
  <resource id="143.155">I guess it's hard to read about a condition when you don't know the name of it.</resource>
  <resource id="143.156">I didn't always have this problem.</resource>
  <resource id="143.157">When I was a kid I slept.</resource>
  <resource id="143.158">I think.</resource>
  <resource id="143.159">Actually, I don't remember much about my childhood.</resource>
  <resource id="143.160">All this not sleeping has a terrible way of playing with your memory.</resource>
  <resource id="143.161">I don't only feel like the days stretch on interminably.</resource>
  <resource id="143.162">They really do when there's no end.</resource>
  <resource id="143.163">Just me in my room, reading my books and wondering what life would be like if I slept.</resource>
  <resource id="143.164">Do I go to work again tomorrow morning?</resource>
  <resource id="143.165">I think I do.</resource>
  <resource id="143.166">It's Sunday, but tomorrow might be a holiday.</resource>
  <resource id="143.167">It is, isn't it?</resource>
  <resource id="143.168">But I think I work on that holiday.</resource>
  <resource id="143.169">Nothing has much definition unless I'm staring at a calendar.</resource>
  <resource id="143.170">I actually keep one everywhere, one in my car, one in each room of my apartment, one in my cubicle.</resource>
  <resource id="143.171">How else would I keep all these days straight?</resource>
  <resource id="143.172">It's not like a sleep in on the weekend.</resource>
  <resource id="143.173">I can't always read about this condition, or whatever it may be.</resource>
  <resource id="143.174">I'm beginning to think there may be nothing to it.</resource>
  <resource id="143.175">I'd tell my doctor, but I don't go to a doctor.</resource>
  <resource id="143.176">Otherwise, I'm a totally healthy male of my species.</resource>
  <resource id="143.177">I weigh about 180 lbs, I'm about 5', 10", I don't have anything that seems to be wrong with me. I have these weird scars on my neck.</resource>
  <resource id="143.178">Something from when I was a kid.</resource>
  <resource id="143.179">An accident.</resource>
  <resource id="143.180">And there's the scar on my lip.</resource>
  <resource id="143.181">I don't know where that came from.</resource>
  <resource id="143.182">I would say it was from some drunken night of excess, but I've never blacked out either.</resource>
  <resource id="143.183">I can drink and drink.</resource>
  <resource id="143.184">Oh, I get drunk, but nothing seems to happen to me where I forget.</resource>
  <resource id="143.185">I remember every second, every horrific moment leaning over a toilet and puking.</resource>
  <resource id="143.186">It's unfair that I can remember things like that so well, yet I can't even tell you much about what happened in the rest of my life.</resource>
  <resource id="143.187">Selective memory, they say.</resource>
  <resource id="143.188">Most times, I can't even remember what I did five seconds ago.</resource>
  <resource id="143.189">You'd think that'd be a problem at work, but it's menial stuff.</resource>
  <resource id="143.190">Doesn't require much of a brain to do it.</resource>
  <resource id="143.191">I spend most of my time in my cube typing and filing, thinking about what I read the night before, trying to process it, trying to figure out what is/has been happening to me. Last night, I read As I Lay Dying by William Faulkner.</resource>
  <resource id="143.192">Have you ever read it?</resource>
  <resource id="143.193">It's kind of confusing.</resource>
  <resource id="143.194">I kept having to reread passages until I figured out that it's not chronological.</resource>
  <resource id="143.195">My favorite character is Vardaman.</resource>
  <resource id="143.196">I think he had a point.</resource>
  <resource id="143.197">But I can't remember it right now.</resource>
  <resource id="143.198">I'm at work, and I left the book at home.</resource>
  <resource id="143.199">I'll have to look when I get home.</resource>
  <resource id="143.200">Unless I returned it to the library.</resource>
  <resource id="143.201">I walk to work.</resource>
  <resource id="143.202">When it's nice.</resource>
  <resource id="143.203">Today it's nice.</resource>
  <resource id="143.204">It's a couple miles, but I don't have a lot of people that I have to rush home to see.</resource>
  <resource id="143.205">Just my books these days.</resource>
  <resource id="143.206">I really need to get more friends.</resource>
  <resource id="143.207">My walk takes me by Lake Calhoun.</resource>
  <resource id="143.208">Most days I look at it as I pass by the fishers, the runners, the dog walkers.</resource>
  <resource id="143.209">Every so often, I walk around it.</resource>
  <resource id="143.210">I don't know what it is about the lake.</resource>
  <resource id="143.211">Some days it just lures me. The sun catches fire in the western sky as I start around the trail.</resource>
  <resource id="143.212">A shelf of clouds cover the sky, and Calhoun, choppy from the breeze, glitters only slightly.</resource>
  <resource id="143.213">Just the way I like it.</resource>
  <resource id="143.214">I itch my neck for some reason it itches.</resource>
  <resource id="143.215">Soon the lake will be frozen, everyone walking on top of it.</resource>
  <resource id="143.216">Man triumphing over nature, something like that.</resource>
  <resource id="143.217">I don't get it.</resource>
  <resource id="143.218">Just like the fisherman.</resource>
  <resource id="143.219">Doesn't seem fair, not at all.</resource>
  <resource id="143.220">I amble around the lake, and the people are slowly thinning out.</resource>
  <resource id="143.221">I guess it's getting colder, and it's getting to be time for dinner, or far enough after work, or whatever.</resource>
  <resource id="143.222">I, on the other hand, have nothing to go home to.</resource>
  <resource id="143.223">I should have brought a book with me. Then I could read at the lake</resource>
  <resource id="143.224">I suppose the cold should annoy me. But it doesn't. I begin to take off my shoes.</resource>
  <resource id="143.225">People tell me that the water in the lakes around here isn't that good.</resource>
  <resource id="143.226">But people swim in this, right?</resource>
  <resource id="143.227">And there are plenty of fish.</resource>
  <resource id="143.228">The water can't be that bad.</resource>
  <resource id="143.229">And it's not that cold yet.</resource>
  <resource id="143.230">For some reason, my skin doesn't get cold and I like the water running between my toes.</resource>
  <resource id="143.231">People stare as I dangle my feet into the lake in October.</resource>
  <resource id="143.232">No one is in the lake, and I think everyone thinks something is wrong with me. Something is wrong me, but I still don't know what.</resource>
  <resource id="143.233">The water swirls around my feet, swirls and I have the sudden compunction to jump in.</resource>
  <resource id="143.234">I control it at first.</resource>
  <resource id="143.235">A person walks by me and says something, but I don't know what, and I'm singing something, but I can't place to tune.</resource>
  <resource id="143.236">Something my mother sang to me to put me to sleep.</resource>
  <resource id="143.237">I think.</resource>
  <resource id="143.238">I could be wrong.</resource>
  <resource id="143.239">But where else would I know it from.</resource>
  <resource id="143.240">I don't listen to music, but I know this song.</resource>
  <resource id="143.241">Vardaman is right.</resource>
  <resource id="143.242">I quickly strip.</resource>
  <resource id="143.243">More people are shouting, but I don't hear them, and I don't look at them.</resource>
