<block id="block 155">
  <resource id="155.691">Have you ever been ambitious to start a new hobby or explore a new discipline, only to be discouraged by the sheer volume of information, sources, and endless tangle around the subject?</resource>
  <resource id="155.692">Even a trip to the book store proves that there is so much material to absorb on a new topic, that you don't even know where to begin!</resource>
  <resource id="155.693">And forget the Internet, where as much as 75% of all information is general nonsense.</resource>
  <resource id="155.694">Wouldn't it be a refreshing switch to know that there is someone out there willing to guide you through an unknown region, in very easy terms, such that you would actually enjoy learning something new?</resource>
  <resource id="155.695">Enter stage left: Daniel's Five Minute Guides.</resource>
  <resource id="155.696">something the Internet has been missing since it was born!</resource>
  <resource id="155.697">Try out this first primer, a quick five minute guide to learning and loving classical music.</resource>
  <resource id="155.698">Five minutes is all you need, so grab a glass of your favorite wine, and enjoy your newfound knowledge!</resource>
  <resource id="155.699">If these prissy little installments are truly helpful, I may just have to take more requests!</resource>
  <resource id="155.700">Classical Music 101</resource>
  <resource id="155.701">Welcome to the world of classical music.</resource>
  <resource id="155.702">It's my world, a place where music in endless forms and varieties give me an entire lifetime of listening pleasure.</resource>
  <resource id="155.703">It's the oldest recognized type of music in Western civilization, and it dates back well into the Middle Ages.</resource>
  <resource id="155.704">Since classical music is the oldest and largest genre of music in existence, let's study this music from one era to the next.</resource>
  <resource id="155.705">Most music historians identify anywhere from four to seven distinct eras in classical music, and since I recognize all seven, let's learn about each one:</resource>
  <resource id="155.706">1.</resource>
  <resource id="155.707">Middle Ages (450 A.D. to 1450 A.D): Shortly after the fall of the Roman Empire, the first era of classical music was born.</resource>
  <resource id="155.708">Our first composers were mostly supported by the church (surprise!), and religion influenced early classical music more than any other source.</resource>
  <resource id="155.709">There was no formal system of notation, making composing very difficult and time-consuming.</resource>
  <resource id="155.710">Gregorian chants were written during these ancient times.</resource>
  <resource id="155.711">2.</resource>
  <resource id="155.712">Renaissance (1450 - 1600): Humans discovered that Earth is not the center of the universe, so classical music changed along with the rest of the arts.</resource>
  <resource id="155.713">Music became more of an expressive art than a science, and music was now being printed, allowing sacred, secular, and instrumental music to spread everywhere.</resource>
  <resource id="155.714">Governments now influenced the spread of the era's music more than the church.</resource>
  <resource id="155.715">Claudio Montiverdi was one of the most prominent composers of this era.</resource>
  <resource id="155.716">3.</resource>
  <resource id="155.717">Baroque (1600-1750): The era of Bach, Handel, and Vivaldi saw the development of almost every modern instrument in use today, particularly the violin, clarinet, and piano.</resource>
  <resource id="155.718">Composers developed the idea of the sonata, the concerto, and the symphony, where great performers (called virtuosos) found enormous success.</resource>
  <resource id="155.719">Composers used counterpoint, dance rhythms and repetition in their music.</resource>
  <resource id="155.720">4.</resource>
  <resource id="155.721">Classical (1750-1825): Yes, this era just happens to have the same name as the whole genre that we're studying, yet it has the shortest time span of any classical music period.</resource>
  <resource id="155.722">Think of this brief period as a launch platform of all the modern music to follow.</resource>
  <resource id="155.723">If you speak to a music snob who claims that this is the only "real" era of classical, then politely extend your middle finger while mooning the person.</resource>
  <resource id="155.724">Just kidding.</resource>
  <resource id="155.725">Anyway, classical music wasn't even called classical music back in this time.</resource>
  <resource id="155.726">You can find nearly all of the most popular composers in this era, namely Mozart and Beethoven (both of whom didn't know they were writing "classical").</resource>
  <resource id="155.727">It's pretty funny if you think about it.</resource>
  <resource id="155.728">They used simple ideas, simple textures, and simple forms, all of which make for great listening material.</resource>
  <resource id="155.729">5.</resource>
  <resource id="155.730">Romantic (1825-1900): Lush and beautiful music was the prevailing theme during most of the 1800s, and music was written to tell stories, to depict the mysteries of nature, or to express the upper reaches of human emotions.</resource>
  <resource id="155.731">Composers of this era such as Tchaikovsky, Brahms, and Chopin wrote music with a wider dynamic range, using a larger variety of instruments.</resource>
  <resource id="155.732">Their melodies were longer and more dramatic, and their harmonies were fuller, richer, and more dissonant.</resource>
  <resource id="155.733">Female composers gained more visibility during this period, such as Clara Schumann.</resource>
  <resource id="155.734">6.</resource>
  <resource id="155.735">20th Century: 1900-1999: Classical music exploded, evolved and splintered faster than it ever had before.</resource>
  <resource id="155.736">New subcategories of classical music were born every year, and more composers were writing music than all previous eras combined.</resource>
  <resource id="155.737">Music of this era was influenced by anything and everything, including world wars, politics, SEX, and cross-pollination by other types of music such as jazz.</resource>
  <resource id="155.738">And anything and everything was possible, from atonal to experimental to electronic.</resource>
  <resource id="155.739">Finally, music could be recorded and distributed widely, creating a burgeoning market for all types of new music.</resource>
  <resource id="155.740">Over one hundred thousand composers fall into this period, including Copland, Stravinsky, and Ravel.</resource>
  <resource id="155.741">Needless to say, neither the church nor the government contributed much to classical music in this century.</resource>
  <resource id="155.742">7.</resource>
  <resource id="155.743">Contemporary Classical: 1970 - 2050 (projected): If the 20th Century period ends in 1999, then what do we call all classical music written thereafter?</resource>
  <resource id="155.744">Welcome to the contemporary classical period, where we see even more subcategories such as the minimalism of Philip Glass, the electric opera by John Corigliano, and the twelve-tone works of John Bilotta, a Bay Area composer!</resource>
  <resource id="155.745">The few creative barriers that may have been left after the 20th century have been crushed by a whopping 300,000 living composers, and this contemporary period gives new meaning to the phrase "Anything Goes!"</resource>
  <resource id="155.746">That was painless, wasn't it?</resource>
  <resource id="155.747">There is so much more to learn, but this is your first step into removing the mystery from these vast eras.</resource>
  <resource id="155.748">Your next few leaps into classical music should now be a little easier!</resource>
  <resource id="155.749">Next week, I will begin serving some of the grandest, most stunning, and most daring classical music the world has seen.</resource>
  <resource id="155.750">The autoplay in each entry will be set to off, but please don't be shy, click play!</resource>
  <resource id="155.751">Add a little passion to your life each time you visit, and share my world with me!</resource>
</block>

<block id="block 156">
  <resource id="156.752">Measure 40 amends the state constitution to require supreme court and appeals judges to be elected by district instead of the statewide vote that currently happens.</resource>
  <resource id="156.753">Arguments in favor: 29</resource>
  <resource id="156.754">Arguments in opposition: 20</resource>
  <resource id="156.755">8 of the "pro" arguments consist of someone listing what I take to be every single county in Oregon from which there are no appellate judges.</resource>
  <resource id="156.756">Both sides have a mixture of more polemic arguments and more reasonable arguments.</resource>
  <resource id="156.757">There's all kinds of thought about how the measure will make judicial positions more or less political, whether those positions already are political, whether it matters that the Oregon constitution originally had district elections and switched to statewide some time ago, whether elections by district mean that some of the judges will be less qualified than others could have been or whether it means that currently underrepresented areas (think every rural area) will have a better chance to have someone who understands them in the appellate courts, etc.</resource>
  <resource id="156.758">All in all, most of the arguments are not very convincing one way or the other.</resource>
  <resource id="156.759">One of the few that seemed to me to say something worth saying was an argument in favor that was written by Ron Saxton.</resource>
  <resource id="156.760">This surprised me, because I know from TV ads that Ron Saxton is running for something or other.</resource>
  <resource id="156.761">As such I consider him to be primarily a politician, and it is rare that I find what I might consider words of wisdom from a politician, even when I agree with them.</resource>
  <resource id="156.762">The one other somewhat-convincing argument was immediately after that one, also in favor, by an organization of Oregon farmers.</resource>
  <resource id="156.763">They talked about how it would be nice if some of the appellate judges understood how farms operate when lawsuits against farmers come up.</resource>
  <resource id="156.764">There seemed to be at least a smidgen of personal experience reflected in that argument, something not really present anywhere else.</resource>
  <resource id="156.765">So I'm leaning slightly in favor, although I mostly don't see how it will make that big of a difference.</resource>
</block>

<block id="block 173">
  <resource id="173.102">I am, by no means, an art critic.</resource>
  <resource id="173.103">I know very little about art, save the few well-known artists that everyone knows.</resource>
  <resource id="173.104">For my birthday, Jon took me to the Getty Center in Los Angeles.</resource>
  <resource id="173.105">For those who don't know, Jean Paul Getty was a big-time oil tycoon here in California who, like a lot of people with insane amounts of money, started buying a lot of art.</resource>
  <resource id="173.106">The Getty Museum in LA stems from his collection.</resource>
  <resource id="173.107">The Getty is this huge, beautiful architectual wonder that sits on the top of a hill that overlooks Westwood and downtown LA. They have everything from Greek statues to modern photography exhibits.</resource>
  <resource id="173.108">They have several Degas.</resource>
  <resource id="173.109">and Rembrants.</resource>
  <resource id="173.110">and Monets.</resource>
  <resource id="173.111">and Reniors.</resource>
  <resource id="173.112">and Titians.</resource>
  <resource id="173.113">and Van Goghs.</resource>
  <resource id="173.114">Now, understand, when it comes right down to it, I am a hick from the sticks.</resource>
  <resource id="173.115">The only "art" I have ever been exposed to is the stuff that pretentious college drop-outs create and try to sell by hanging it on the walls for coffee shops.</resource>
  <resource id="173.116">I had never before seen a work by any of the Masters - the closest I have ever gotten to a Michelangelo is watching "Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles".</resource>
  <resource id="173.117">It's easy to Google Image search "Monet" and look at the results, but pictures and posters hold no candle to the real deal.</resource>
  <resource id="173.118">I cried (yes, tears streaming down the face like a big dork) when I saw Rembrant's "Old Man in a Military Costume."</resource>
  <resource id="173.119">I cried again in the Impressionists Gallery.</resource>
  <resource id="173.120">I was so amazed by the brush strokes, the colors, the view up close and stepping back.</resource>
  <resource id="173.121">I saw a real Bouguereau.</resource>
  <resource id="173.122">A real Alma-Tadema.</resource>
  <resource id="173.123">And all I could do was blubber like a sap at the beauty.</resource>
  <resource id="173.124">Oh, and I think I made the security guards really nervous - like I was going to blow my runny nose on the Renoir and forever ruin it with my bodily fluids.</resource>
  <resource id="173.125">They didn't like me very much.</resource>
  <resource id="173.126">Photobucket - Video and Image Hosting</resource>
  <resource id="173.127">(Pretty, no? You should see it in person!)</resource>
  <resource id="173.128">So, I'm sure you're asking, "What on earth is this crazy broad getting at?!</resource>
  <resource id="173.129">We already know she's a big doofus - tell us something we didn't know!"</resource>
  <resource id="173.130">Well, I'm getting to that!</resource>
  <resource id="173.131">Like I said, I'm no judge of art.</resource>
  <resource id="173.132">Back in June, Jon and I made a quick spur-of-the-moment trip to the Denver Museum of Nature and Science.</resource>
  <resource id="173.133">The purpose - to catch Body Worlds 2 while we could (the exhibit had already come and gone to LA, so this was the only chance we would get.)</resource>
  <resource id="173.134">I was excited to see it as I have a fondness of cadavers (blame Gross Anatomy) and really wanted to get another shot at some up close anatomy viewing.</resource>
  <resource id="173.135">Luckily for me, Jon is great about indulging my whims.</resource>
  <resource id="173.136">Photobucket - Video and Image Hosting</resource>
  <resource id="173.137">Even though I had gotten tickets online and reserved our place, the wait was long.</resource>
  <resource id="173.138">The exhibit itself was packed - they were at capacity at all times, and moving along the initial cases of bones and joints became tiresome (my response to the situation was to tell Jon to take all the time he liked to view things, while I moved more quickly through things - "yeah, a femur.</resource>
  <resource id="173.139">Another femur.</resource>
  <resource id="173.140">A femoralacetabular joint.</resource>
  <resource id="173.141">A glenoid-humoral joint.") Some of the exhibits were very interesting, such as  the soccer player and the skateboarder.</resource>
  <resource id="173.142">In Gross Anatomy, our cadavers were solemn - laying on the tables as if in a deep slumber.</resource>
  <resource id="173.143">Seeing the body in action was very interesting.</resource>
  <resource id="173.144">Also interesting were the cases of organs - healthy and diseased.</resource>
  <resource id="173.145">However, the entire exhibit wasn't as cut and dry (excuse the horrible pun.)</resource>
  <resource id="173.146">The odd thing about Body Worlds is that it goes from being an anatomy exhibit to being a sort of Cadaver Carnival of Bizarre Art.</resource>
  <resource id="173.147">The cadavers are flayed open in weird completely un-anatomical positions, or skinned (yes, horrible word, but it's one we actually use in lab) in such a way that the grotesque imagery overshadows the actual anatomy.</resource>
  <resource id="173.148">(I can't find any good images of the weird displays, so to view some you have to look here at the International Body Worlds Online Store.)</resource>
  <resource id="173.149">Again, I am no judge of art, and the fact that this exhibit has drawn record numbers of people hint at the possibility that I am off-base.</resource>
  <resource id="173.150">Body Worlds is great in that it gives everyday people the chance to look at the machinery that is the body.</resource>
  <resource id="173.151">Now, people can see that organs are not the cartoonish objects that one sees in a book.</resource>
  <resource id="173.152">The general public also has a chance to look at diseased organs (something usually reserved for pathologists, who, I've noticed, seem to get *very* excited over things like polyps and necrosis.)</resource>
  <resource id="173.153">They have the blood vessels of various limbs that have been injected with polymers so that you can witness the intricacies of the blood supply.</resource>
  <resource id="173.154">The basic anatomy and bodies in motion portions are a must see.</resource>
  <resource id="173.155">However, for me, the more "artistic" exhibits were more of a turn off - I willingly admit that I was very attached to my cadaver in Gross, and the thought of him being displayed like that makes me a bit sick.</resource>
  <resource id="173.156">Body Worlds is both fascinating and repelling.</resource>
  <resource id="173.157">Then again, I'm not a good judge of art.</resource>
  <resource id="173.158">As an aside, there have been some interesting articles regarding the aquisition of cadavers for exhibits such as Body Worlds (I guess the entire affair has sparked various "knock-off" exhibits!)</resource>
  <resource id="173.159">Due to increased demand for bodies, China has stepped into the body buisness.</resource>
  <resource id="173.160">While this country has access to huge numbers of scientists and medical students (someone has to do the dirty work) there have been some controversies, and questions as to where the cadavers come from.</resource>
  <resource id="173.161">The creator of "Body Worlds" has denied that his specimines have been acquired in any dubious manner, and has documentation to that effect.</resource>
  <resource id="173.162">However, some of the knock-offs might not be as professional (personally, I'd avoid them if I were you.)</resource>
  <resource id="173.163">I won't make any more comments about it, and leave it in your hands.</resource>
</block>
